The game I'd like to show tonight - assuming I can stay awake, anyway - features this "actualisation of what was only a static avatar," from a different generation.  I will certainly not call any of them a #Vtuber. But the idea is there.
That broader idea of avatars, as I said previously, is why I refuse to think of Ami Yamato as a #Vtuber. She's from an older generation, and it makes as much sense as calling Narnia an Isekai.
My main idea of what a #Vtuber is, is that it is an actualisation of what was once a static, forum avatar. The rest is up to you.  That's why I don't really care about Kizuna Ai that much. She's an idol.  But I also worry about getting along with anyone. It's a much broader idea.

That's why I started with some specific steps. Make sure you have a clear idea on what you believe a #Vtuber is, and make sure you own it. Make it yours.  But it's not easy. I, for one, am constantly troubled on a total lack of viewers and comments. I've been avoiding everyone.
This #Vtuber thing is a new creative space. Lots of people are going to try, lots of people are going to drop out. Even the first ones who did this are vulnerable, despite an early advantage. That's what happened in the dot-com and video game bubbles.
When I brought this up last night, it was mentioned that a lot of "unusual" stuff was happening in the #Vtuber space recently.  But all I can really say in response to that, is that this whole thing is a bubble.

While VTuber (with an uppercase T) refers stylistically to the "YouTube" brand, Vtuber (with a lowercase t) would theoretically no longer do so. For this reason, the term "Vtuber" should be trademark-free and not associated with any Google trademarks.
Today, i would like to share some thoughts on the current discussion about the stylization of the term "Vtuber". These are only my personal thoughts and do not have to agree with the current legal situation.
